For we find that many such persons as Paiá¹­hÄ«nasi, BaudhÄyana, Pracetas and the rest are recognised by the wise and learned as reliable â€˜Recollectors,â€™ and yet these names are not found in any of the lists (supplied by various Smá¹›tis).

What thus the words â€˜Smá¹›tiÅ›Ä«le ca tadvidÄmâ€™ mean is that â€˜when a person is found to be recognised and spoken of by all wise and learned persons as endowed with the said qualifications, and they also accept a certain work as really by that person,â€”the word of such a person (and of the work composed by him), even though proceeding from a human source, should be recognised as an authoritative source of the knowledge of Dharma. So that even at the present day if there were a person possessed of the said qualifications, and he were to compose a work by reason of just those qualifications, then for later generations they would be accepted to be just as authoritative as the words of Manu and others. People of the present generationâ€”who would be contemporaries of the said writerâ€”would not derive their knowledge of Dharma from the words of such a writer, because the sources of information available to him would be all available to them also. Hence it is that until a teacher of the present day clearly indicates the source from which he has derived a certain information, learned people do not accept his word as reliable. When however he has pointed out his source and his work has been accepted as authoritative, then at some future time if the case of his work be found to be analogous to that of the Smá¹›ti rules regarding Aá¹£á¹­akÄ and other acts (whose basis in the Veda we of the present day cannot find), it would be only right to infer its authoritative character from the fact of its being accepted by the wise and the learned (which fact could not be explained except on the basis of its being duly authoritative).

â€˜Ä€cÄrascaiva sÄdhÅ«nÄmâ€™ â€˜The practice of Good Menâ€™;â€”the particle â€˜caâ€™ connects the epithet â€˜vedavidÄmâ€™ (of persons versed in the Veda) with this phrase also. It is not possible to collect in any compilation all such practices, there being endless forms of them, varying with the diversities in the nature of men, caused by such variable circumstances as the calm or disturbed condition of their mind and so forth. The same act that may have been found, on several, occasions, to be pleasing (to one person), may, on another occasion, turn out to be unpleasant (to another person). (1) The Injunction or Mandatory textâ€”e.g., â€˜one shall sacrifice the goat to YÄyuâ€™â€”this is a trustworthy guide as to what one should do.

(2) The Prohibitive Textâ€”e.g., â€˜one shall not eat the flesh of an animal killed by the poisoned arrow this is a guide as to what one shall avoid.

(3) The â€˜Declamatoryâ€™ text of two kinds: the commendatory and the condemnatory; the former serves the purpose of delineating the excellence of the course of action enjoined by the Mandatory text; e.g., the text â€˜VÄyu is the eftest deity,â€™ serves to indicate the excellent properties of the deity VÄyu to whom the offering of the goat has been enjoined; the condemnatory text serves to deprecate the course of action prohibited; e.g., the assertion that â€˜the tears of weeping Rudra became silverâ€™ is meant to deprecate the giving of silver as the sacrificial fee, which has been forbidden by a prohibitive text. Texts of this declamatory kind are of use sometimes in settling a doubtful point: e.g., it having been enjoined that one should place wet pebbles under the altar, and the injunction being silent as to the substance with which the pebble is to be wetted, the doubt on this point is settled by a subsequent â€˜declamatoryâ€™ text, â€˜clarified butter is glory itself,â€™ which clearly indicates the clarified butter as the substance with which the pebbles are to be wetted.

(4) The Mantra textâ€”e.g., â€˜Devasya tvÄ savituá¸¥,â€™ etc., serves to remind the performer of the details of the performance in the shape of the deity and so forth.

(5) The proper names of particular sacrifices help in the determining of the exact action connoted by the common root â€˜yaji,â€™ â€˜to sacrificeâ€™ occurring in the injunctive text.

(6) The meaningless syllables, stobhas, introduced in the SÄma-chant, serve the purpose of marking time and cadence;

and (7) the Upaniá¹£ad text serves to promulgate that knowledge of Brahman which destroys all evil.

ParÄÅ›ara, 1-20â€”speaks of the â€˜propounders of Åšrutiâ€™ appearing at the beginning of each kalpa. From the words it would seem as if the three godsâ€”BrahmÄ, Viá¹£á¹‡u and MaheÅ›vara were the said â€˜propounders.â€™ But MÄdhava (p. 98) takes the â€˜propounders of Åšruti, Smá¹›ti and SadÄchÄraâ€™ separately from BrahmÄ, etc., and he supplies a peculiar account of the â€˜propounders of Åšrutiâ€™â€”which extends the scope of the authority of this source of knowledge. MedhÄtithi on Manu, 2.6.â€”â€˜Smá¹›tiâ€™ is Recollection and â€˜ÅšÄ«laâ€™ denotes freedom from love, hatred and such improper feelings; this latter, according to one explanation, is a means of accomplishing Dharma, and not a means of knowing it; and it has been separately mentioned in the present connection only with a view to emphasise its importance.   3. and he must be â€˜righteousâ€™ (â€˜SÄdhÅ«mÄmâ€™), be habitually engaged in carrying out the injunctions of the Veda.

Ná¹›siá¹ƒhaprasÄda-Saá¹ƒskÄra MSS.â€”â€œHow can any authority attach to the Smá¹›tis of Manu and others, which being of human origin are open to the suspicion of the possibility of all those defects to which human writers are liable; and for this reason these cannot be regarded as authoritative in the same manner as the Vedas are, whose authority is above suspicion.â€â€”The answer to this is that inasmuch as these Smá¹›tis are found to be mere reproductions of what is contained in the Veda, they must be regarded as duly authoritative. ####### (C) SadÄcÄra

MedhÄtithi on Manu, 2-6.â€”â€˜SÄdhÅ«nÄm ÄchÄraá¸¥,â€™ â€˜Practice of good menâ€™ also has to be construed with â€˜VedavidÄmâ€™ â€˜learned in the Veda,â€™ and the two qualifications â€˜Goodnessâ€™ and â€˜Vedic learning,â€™ come under â€˜culture.â€™ When in regard to any action, there are no Vedic or Smá¹›ti declarations available, but cultured people are found to do it as â€˜Dharma,â€™â€”something rightâ€”then that action is to be regarded as â€˜enjoined in the Vedaâ€™ in the same manner as anything laid down in the Smá¹›ti. What are meant by â€˜practicesâ€™ here are such customs as the tying of the bracelet at marriage, the keeping of an exact number of hair-locks on the head, the exact manner of receiving guests and so forth. Each of such practices has to be taken on its own merits; it is not possible to assume Vedic texts corroborating those ither severally or even collectively; as the rightness or wrongness of a certain practice varies with circumstances; e.g., a certain cultured man may be very assiduous in attending upon his guestsâ€”never leaving them for a single moment unattended, and so forth. This may he quite agreeable to one guest who likes constant attendance; but there may be another to whom all this close attendance is disagreeable; he would prefer much rather to be left alone to himself. There is no such variation possible in regard to what is prescribed in the Smá¹›ti; and herein lies the difference between what is prescribed in the â€˜Smá¹›tiâ€™ and what can be learnt from the â€˜Practices of the cultured.â€™

Manu, 2.10.â€”â€˜The Veda is to be known as Åšruti, R evealed Word, and the DharmaÅ›Ästra, Legal Ordinances, as Smá¹›ti; in all matters these two do not deserve to be criticised.â€™

VÄ«ramitrodaya-ParibhÄá¹£Ä, pp. 25-29.â€”When there is a conflict between two Vedic texts, both are to be regarded as equally authoritative, and in actual practice the two courses of action are to be treated as optional alternatives. There is a further distinction among authorities of the same class also. For instance, between two Vedic texts, if one is of doubtful import while the meaning of the other is clear, then the latter is to be given preference. If one treats of a more important matter than the other, it is to be given preference; e.g., if one deals with something to be done, while the other, with a minor detail of the act, the former is to set aside the latter. What occurs in the opening sentence sets aside what occurs in the concluding sentence; e.g., the opening sentence speaks of the â€˜three Vedas,â€™ and the concluding sentence speaks of the â€˜á¹šk Verseâ€™ as to he recited â€˜loudly,â€™ the signification of the word â€˜á¹škâ€™ in the latter is sublated by that of the word â€˜Vedaâ€™in the former, and the â€˜loudnessâ€™ becomes connected with the three Vedas, and not with the Mantras of the á¹šk Veda only. If what is said in one text is beset with more difficulties than what occurs in another, then the former is to be rejected; e.g., one text lays down that a man who accepts the gift of horses should perform as many sacrifices as the horses he receives; while another speaks of PrajÄpati having given a horse to Varuá¹‡a and performed a sacrifice to this deityâ€”by which the giver and not the receiver, is to perform the sacrifice;â€”now if we accept the former, i.e., if we accept the view that the sacrifice is to be performed by the receiver of the gift, then we shall have the following difficulties in the construing of the latter text: (1) it will be necessary to take the Dative in â€˜Varuá¹‡Äyaâ€™ (â€˜to Varuá¹‡aâ€™) as standing for the Ablative (â€˜from Varuá¹‡aâ€™), and (2) to take the verb â€˜givenâ€™ as standing for â€˜acceptedâ€™; while on the other hand, if we accept the latter text, according to which the sacrifice is to be performed by the giver of the horse, then the only difficulty involved in the construing of the former text is to take the verb â€˜acceptâ€™ as standing for â€˜giveâ€™; and hence it is the former text that is rejected, and the conclusion is that the giver of a horse is to perform sacrifices. Where one thing is enjoined in connection with a particular Veda, this is given preference over what would apply to that same, in accordance with what has been prescribed in connection with another Veda; e.g., in connection with the Yajurveda we have the injunction that its Mantras are to be recited in an undertone; in accordance with this rule one may be led to think that the chanting of the VÄravantÄ«ya (SÄma-hymn) is to be done in an undertone; but this is precluded by the â€˜high pitchâ€™ that has been enjoined in connection with the chanting of the mantras of the SÄma-Veda. What is enjoined in regard to the particular â€˜Vedic Recensional Schoolâ€™ to which the performerâ€™s family belongs is given preference over what may have been enjoined in connection with another school. The general law is superseded by the particular, and so on.

In the case of Smá¹›tis also,â€”the â€˜orthodoxâ€™ Smá¹›ti sets aside the â€˜heterodox,â€™â€”among the â€˜orthodoxâ€™ Smá¹›tis also, if a certain course of action is sanctioned by one, hut condemned by another, the latter sets aside the former, and the action in question is to he avoided. What is laid down in reference to imperceptible transcendental effects sets aside what is enjoined for temporal or worldly ends; e.g., the law prohibiting the killing of the BrÄhmaá¹‡a sets aside the law that â€˜one may kill a person who is threatening to kill,â€™ as the latter course of action is meant only to serve the perceptible purpose of saving oneâ€™s life.. The Smá¹›ti that is based upon a Vedic ArthavÄda is set aside by that which is based upon a Vedic injunction; e.g., the law sanctioning the killing of the cow in honour of an honoured guest,â€”being based upon an ArthavÄda passage commendatory of the â€˜churningâ€™ or â€˜rubbingâ€™ of sticks,â€”is set aside by that which prohibits the said killing,â€”this latter being based upon the Vedic injunctive passageâ€”â€˜Kill not Aditi, the hornless cow.â€™

Among â€˜Custom,â€™ â€˜self-satisfactionâ€™ and â€˜well considered vow,â€™â€”the preceding is more authoritative than the following; as declared by the MitÄká¹£arÄ.

In some cases what is sanctioned by higher authority is rejected by what is said by a lower authority; e.g., the drinking of wine (at the SautrÄmaá¹‡i sacrifice), even though enjoined by the Veda, is not considered right, in view of its prohibition during Kali - Yuga contained in the Smá¹›ti.

Any Smá¹›ti that goes against the ordinances of Manu is to be rejectedâ€”as declared by Bá¹›haspati (see above). This is the view of the Kalpataru also.â€”Customs, local, tribal as well as family, are to be rejected if repugnant to the Veda or the Smá¹›ti or the PurÄá¹‡a.

As between Åšruti and Smá¹›ti, the conclusion arrived at is thus expressed by VyÄsa:â€”â€˜That law which is deduced from the Veda is the higher, while that declared in the PurÄá¹‡a and other Smá¹›tis is the lower; which means that in cases of conflict our first duty is to do what is laid down in the Veda, and the doing of what is declared in the Smá¹›ti can be justified only as a substitute, i.e., to be adopted only when there is no possibility of the other being adopted. And this for the simple reason that according to Manu (11.30), if one follows the â€˜second bestâ€™ course when the best course is possible, his action becomes futile; so that tho conclusion indicated by this is that even in cases of conflict tho Smá¹›ti does not entirely lose its authoritative character; all that happens is that the course of action sanctioned by it is rendered fruitless by reason of the superiority of authority attaching to the Vedic text to the contrary.

The variability of the Law is unfeignedly declared by ParÄÅ›ara in 1.22:â€”

â€˜The Law or the Right is one in the Ká¹›ta Cycle, different in the TretÄ Cycle, yet different in the DvÄpara Cycle, and yet different in the Kali Cycle,â€”varying as it does with the character of the Time-Cycles.â€™

On this MÄdhava makes the following observations:â€”

The â€˜differenceâ€™ spoken of here is, not of the nature or essence, of the Law or Right, but of its modes. If it were the former, then it would imply a corresponding diversity in the Veda also, as the source of that Law; while as a matter of fact, the Veda does not vary with the time-cycles. As regards the modes however, we have several instances of diversity; for instance, though the act of the Agnihotra- offering itself is the same, yet there is diversity in regard to the mode of performing it according as it is performed in the morning or in the evening. For instance, at the evening-performance the sprinkling is to be done with the mantra â€˜á¹štantva satyena pariá¹£iÃ±chÄmi,â€™ while that at the morning-performance with the mantra â€˜Satyantva á¹›tena pariá¹£iÃ±chÄmi.â€™ Thus in the present instance also, the variation lies in the mode of doing what is â€˜right,â€™ and not in what is â€˜rightâ€™ itself; the variations being due to the nature of the time-cycle and of the capacity of the man doing the acts. This matter has been fully discussed in the MÄ«mÄá¹ƒsÄ-SÅ«tra VI.3, where the conclusion arrived at is that in the case of the Agnihotra and such other obligatory rites, only those prescribed details have to be performed which it is within the capacity of the performer to perform. BaudhÄyana also has declared that the obligatory acts are to be performed to the extent that one can; they should, on no account, be entirely omitted.

The most important instance of variation is cited by ParÄÅ›ara (1.23) himselfâ€”â€œIn the Ká¹›ta Cycle, Austerity is the highest Dharma or Duty;â€”in the TretÄ, Learning;â€”in the DvÄpara, Sacrifice,â€”and in the Kali, Charity.â€ To the last Bá¹›haspati adds â€˜sympathy and self-control.â€™

There is variation, according to ParÄÅ›ara (1.24), not only in Law, but also in the authority:â€”â€˜Duringthe Ká¹›a, the Laws are those ordained by Manu,â€”during the TretÄ, those ordained by Gautama,â€”during the DvÄpara, those ordained by Åšaá¹…kha-Likhita,â€”and during the Kali, those ordained by ParÄÅ›ara.â€™ This distinction however has never been observed in actual practice, as even up to the present time, the work of Manu holds the highest position among the Smá¹›tis.

####### Conclusion

From the above we conclude that all the authorities are agreed on the following pointsâ€”(a) The Veda is the first and paramount authority, (b) The Smá¹›ti is authoritative only in so far as it is not repugnant to the Veda, to which it owes its authority; but only on matters on which we have no paramount authority, (c) Practices or Customs are trustworthy guides, only as they are current among the â€˜cultured,â€™ and then too only those that are not repugnant to Vedic or Smá¹›ti texts. (d) The same with regard to Tribal or Family Customs. (e) The judgment of the â€˜Assemblyâ€™ of the learned is to be accepted as authoritative only when it is not repugnant to the Veda, and only when tho judgment is â€˜unbiasedâ€™ by improper feelings. There is not a single text, or â€˜explanation,â€™ which favours the opinion that Custom is to override original texts,â€”an opinion that has been upheld by the Privy Council, and endorsed by eminent writers on Anglo-Hindu Law. Neither VijÃ±ÄneÅ›vara (MitÄká¹£arÄ) nor JÄ«mÅ«tavÄhana (Dharmaratna) nor NÄ«lakaá¹‡á¹­ha (MayÅ«kha) countenances any such view; and these three are regarded by our lawyers as the founders of the principal â€˜Schools of Law.â€™

â€œScripture is not authoritative; why then should contrariness to it be regarded as undesirable? That the scripture (Veda) is unauthoritative is proved by such discrepancies as (a) untruthfulness, (b) inconsistency and (c) repetition.

(a) Such sacrifices as the KÄrÄ«rÄ« (which is laid down as to be performed for obtaining rain) are performed by men desiring min, but as a matter of fact no rain comes after the performance. à¤¯à¤šà¥ à¤šà¥‹à¤•à¥à¤¤à¤‚ à¤¹à¤¿à¤‚à¤¸à¤¾à¤¯à¤¾à¤‚ à¤²à¥‹à¤•à¤µà¥‡à¤¦à¤¯à¥‹à¤°à¥ à¤¨ à¤µà¤¿à¤¶à¥‡à¤· à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿, à¤¤à¤¤à¥à¤° à¤¶à¤¾à¤¸à¥à¤¤à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤µà¤—à¤®à¥à¤¯à¥‹ à¤¹à¤¿ à¤¤à¤¸à¥à¤¯à¤¾ à¤…à¤¯à¤‚ à¤¸à¥à¤µà¤­à¤¾à¤µà¤ƒ, à¤¨ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤•à¥à¤·à¤¾à¤¦à¤¿à¤—à¥‹à¤šà¤°à¤ƒ à¥¤ à¤¤à¤¤à¥à¤° à¤š à¤­à¥‡à¤¦à¤ƒ à¥¤ à¤°à¤¾à¤—à¤²à¤•à¥à¤·à¤£à¤¾ à¤²à¥Œà¤•à¤¿à¤•à¥€ à¤¹à¤¿à¤‚à¤¸à¤¾, à¤µà¤¿à¤§à¤¿à¤²à¤•à¥à¤·à¤£à¤¾à¤²à¥Œà¤•à¤¿à¤•à¥€ à¤¹à¤¿à¤‚à¤¸à¤¾ à¥¤ à¤µà¤¿à¤§à¤¿à¤²à¤•à¥à¤·à¤£à¤¾ à¤¤à¥à¤µà¥ à¤…à¤—à¥à¤¨à¥€à¤·à¥‹à¤®à¥€à¤¯à¤¸à¥à¤¯à¥‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¤®à¤¹à¤¾à¤¨à¥ à¤­à¥‡à¤¦à¤ƒ à¥¤ à¤¤à¤¸à¥à¤®à¤¾à¤¨à¥ à¤¨ à¤•à¤¿à¤‚à¤šà¤¿à¤¦à¥ à¤µà¥‡à¤¦à¥‡ à¤½à¤¨à¥ƒà¤¤à¤®à¥ à¥¤ à¤µà¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤˜à¤¾à¤¤à¤‚ à¤ªà¤°à¤¸à¥à¤¤à¤¾à¤¤à¥ à¤ªà¤°à¤¿à¤¹à¤°à¤¿à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¶à¥à¤²à¥‹à¤•à¥‡à¤¨à¥ˆà¤µ à¥¥ à¥¨.à¥§à¥¦ à¥¥

à¤—à¤™à¥à¤—à¤¾à¤¨à¤¥-à¤­à¤¾à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤¦à¤ƒ

Objection:â€”â€œIs this a treatise on the meanings of words, a lexicon,â€”like the works beginning with such words as Ä€tmabhÅ«á¸¥, Parameá¹£á¹­hÄ« (are the names of BrahmÄ), and so forth,â€”that it is stated that â€˜Revealed Wordâ€™ means the Veda, and â€˜Recollectionsâ€™ means the DharmaÅ›Ästra?â€

Answer:â€”In ordinary life, the â€˜Practices of Cultured Menâ€™ are not regarded either as â€˜Revealed Wordâ€™ or as â€˜Recollection,â€™ on the ground of their being not codified; codified treatises alone are known as â€˜Smá¹›tis,â€™ â€˜Recollectionsâ€™; and it is for the purpose of declaring that these Practices also are included under â€˜Smá¹›tiâ€™ that the author has set forth this verse.

â€˜DharmaÅ›Ästra,â€™ â€˜Dharma-ordinance,â€™ is that which serves the purpose of â€˜ordainingâ€™ (teaching) Dharma as to be done; and â€˜Smá¹›tiâ€™ is that wherein Dharma is taught, i.e., laid down as to be done; and codification or non-codification is entirely immaterial. Now as a matter of fact a knowledge of what should be done is derived from the Practices of Cultured Men also; so that these also come under â€˜Smá¹›ti.â€™ Hence whenever mention is made of â€˜Smá¹›tiâ€™ in connection with any matter, the Practices of Cultured Men should also be taken as included under the name.

â€œIf all DharmaÅ›Ästra (â€˜ordinance of Dharmaâ€™) is â€˜Smá¹›tiâ€™ then the Veda also, which is the â€˜ordinance of Dharmaâ€™ par excellence, would have to be regarded as Smá¹›ti,â€â€”with a view to preclude the possibility of such an idea being entertained, the author has saidâ€”â€˜The Veda should be known as the Revealed Word.â€™ Where the words conveying the â€˜Teaching of Dharmaâ€™ are directly perceived (heard), it is the â€˜Revealed Wordâ€™; while where the words of Teaching are only recollected, it is â€˜Smá¹›tiâ€™; and since this latter condition is also fulfilled by the â€˜Practices of Cultured Men,â€™ this latter also comes under â€˜Smá¹›tiâ€™; as a matter of fact, no authority can attach to any Practice, in corroboration whereof a Vedic text is not â€˜recollected.â€™

Or, the mention of the â€˜Revealed Wordâ€™ may be explained as serving the purpose of showing that the Smá¹›ti is equal to the Veda.

Question:â€”â€œWhat is that common function of Revealed Word and Recollection which rhe present verse seeks to attribute to the Practices of Cultured Men?â€

Answer:â€”â€˜In all matters these two should not be criticisedâ€™;â€”â€˜These twoâ€™â€”i.e., Revealed Word and Recollection.â€”â€˜In all mattersâ€™â€”i.e., even in regard to apparently inconceivable things, such as are entirely beyond the scope of those means of knowledge that are applicable to perceptible things; e.g., (a) the same act of killing leads, in one case, to good, and while in another case it leads to sin; (b) the drinking of wine leads to Hell, while the drinking of Soma removes sin. In such matters, we should not proceed to discuss the various pros and cons. â€˜Criticismâ€™ consists in raising doubts and conceiving of contrary views. For exampleâ€”â€œIf the act of killing is sinful, then since the act of killing is the same in all cases, that done in the course of Vedic sacrifices should also be sinful;â€”if the latter killing is a source of good, ordinary killing also should be conducive to good; the act being exactly the same in both cases.â€

What is prohibited here is that â€˜criticism,â€™ in which we conceive of the form of an act to be quite the reverse of what is declared in the Veda, and proceeding to examine it by means of reasonings based upon false premisses, begin to insist on the conclusion thus arrived at. It is not meant to prohibit such enquiry and discussion as to whether the Prima Facie View or the Established Thesis is in due accord with the Veda. That such an inquiry is not meant to be prohibited is clear from what the author says later onâ€”â€˜He alone, and none else, knows Dharma, who examines it by reasonings.â€™ (Manu, 12.106)

Question:â€”â€œIs this criticism prohibited with a view to some invisible (superphysical) results?â€

We sayâ€”no. Because it was out of these two that Dharma shone forth. [This is what is intended by the said prohibition.]

This assertion points out the fact that all the arguments, set forth by casuists in support of things contrary to what is laid down in the Veda, are fallacious. These arguments are of the following kindâ€”â€œThe killing of animals in the course of Vedic sacrifices must be sinful, because it is killing, like any ordinary killing.â€â€”Now that killing is sinful is learnt from no other source of knowledge except scriptures; under the circumstances, no reason can be found to establish the sinfulness of killing until the scriptures have been accepted as authoritative; and when once the authority of the Veda has been admitted, it could not be reasonable to bring forward arguments against it, as this would invalidate the (acknowledged) authority of scriptures; and this would involve self-contradiction: at first the scriptures were admitted to be authoritative, and then subsequently they are held to be un-authoritative; and this opinion would be contrary to the personâ€™s own previous assertion,â€”no casuist ever says â€˜my mother is childlessâ€™; and it is also contrary to the scriptures.

The Casuist might argue as follows:â€”

â€œScripture is not authoritative; why then should contrariness to it be regarded as undesirable? That the scripture (Veda) is unauthoritative is proved by such discrepancies as (a) untruthfulness, (b) inconsistency and (c) repetition.

(a) Such sacrifices as the KÄrÄ«rÄ« (which is laid down as to be performed for obtaining rain) are performed by men desiring min, but as a matter of fact no rain comes after the performance. As regards the rain that might come at some future time, it has been well saidâ€”â€˜The KÄrÄ«rÄ« having been performed during the autumn, when the cornfields were drying up, if the rain falls during the spring, this only leads to cattle-disease!â€™ Further, as regards the Jyotiá¹£á¹­oma and such other sacrifices, which are spoken of as bringing their rewards at some future time, since the acts will have completely disappeared after performance, the assertion that their reward would come after a hundred years would be exactly like the confident assertion of the Vampirist. From this it is clear that the Veda is untruthful,

(b) There is â€˜inconsistencyâ€™ also: when it is said â€˜libations should be offered after sunrise,â€™ if it were offered before sunrise, it would be clearly wrong; as it is saidâ€”â€˜those who perform the Agnihotra before sunrise utter falsehood morning after morning.â€™ Then again, it is said â€˜that the libations should be offered before the sun has risen,â€™ for (it is said) â€˜the offering made after sunrise would be like the offering of reception to the guest after he had gone.â€™ Now in the former we have the injunction of offering after sunrise, and a deprecation of offering before sunrise, while in the latter we have the reverse. So that people are always in doubt as to which alternative they should adopt.

(c) The same Agnihotra that is enjoined in one Vedic Rescension is found to be enjoined in another Rescension also; and it has been held (by the MÄ«mÄmsakas) that the act, mentioned in the various texts, is one and the same (Agnihotra) And this is a clear Repetition.â€

That there is no â€˜untruthfulnessâ€™ in the Veda is what is meant by the last quarter of the Verse (â€˜it was out of these that Dharma shone forthâ€™). Because out of the Veda â€˜Dharma aloneâ€”i.e., only that a certain act should be done, in the form of sacrifice,â€”â€˜shone forth,â€™ is expounded; and it does not say anything definite in regard to the time at which the rewards shall appear; this is clear from the fact that the passages that speak of rewards do not make mention of any time; all that we learn from the Injunction is that a certain result shall follow, and the Injunction does not specify the time. As a matter of fact, divisions of time, past, present and future, are related to what is expressed by the verbal root; while the Result is not denoted by the verbal root at all; it is only implied by the Injunction; what is denoted by the verbal root (i.e., the act of â€˜sacrificeâ€™) is actually accomplished at the time (of the performance), in the form of the offering of a substance for the benefit of a certain deity,â€”the fulfilment of this offering appearing in the form of the transformation of the substance offered (into the fire, for instance). Further in ordinary life also, we find that when a person, who is an obedient servant of another, is directed to go to a certain place, he at once obeys the order; though as regards his wages, in some cases he may obtain it at the very beginning; but also sometimes during the act, or even after the act has been accomplished; and then also he may get them on the same day, or the next day, or at some future time. In the same manner, there is no limit as to the time at which the results spoken of in the scriptures will appear; all that is meant is that (by the performance of the act) the result, in the shape of Heaven, Rain or so forth, is brought within reach,â€”and not (hat they appear on the very same day. Then again, just as there are obstacles in the way of the realising of results of acts done in the ordinary course of life, so there are also in the case of the acts prescribed by the Veda,â€”such obstacles consisting of past sins and so forth. This (possibility of the Rain not coming immediately after the act) is clearly shown in the Veda itself when it says â€˜if the rain should not come, the man should continue as before.â€™ And as regards the SarvasvÄra sacrifice (which is laid down as leading the performer to heaven), people have explained that the reward does not consist in the immediate entrance to Heaven, in fact it consists in what the man actually desires, and the desire is in the form â€˜may I reach heaven without difficulty [ i.e., after death; immediate translation to heaven would mean immediate death, which no man desires].

As regards the argument that there is no difference in the act of killing as done in ordinary life and as done during a Vedic sacrifice,â€”what has to be borne in mind is the fact that the sinfulness of the act of killing is known only from the scriptures, it is not amenable to perception or any ordinary means of knowledge; and there is certainly a difference: the ordinary killing is prompted by passion, while the sacrificial killing is prompted by Vedic Injunction; and as the killing of the animal offered to Agni-Soma is prompted by the Vedic Injunction, this constitutes a great difference. From all this it is clear that in the Veda there is nothing â€˜untruthful.â€™

As regards â€˜Inconsistencyâ€™ (which is the second point urged against the Veda), the Author is going to answer it in the text itself (Verses 14-15 below).â€”(10)

On the ground of â€˜untruthfulnessâ€™ and â€˜unreliabilityâ€™ if a twice-born person, relying upon the science of dialectics the â€˜science of dialecticsâ€™ here stands for the polemical works written by Atheists, treatises of Bauddhas and CharvÄkas, in which it is repeatedly proclaimed that â€œthe Veda is conducive to sinâ€;â€”relying upon such a science, if one should scorn the Veda; i.e., when advised by some one to desist from a certain course of action which is sinful according to the Veda and the Smá¹›ti, in the wordsâ€”â€˜Do not do this, it is prohibited by the Veda,â€™â€”if he disregards this advice and persists in doing it, saying, â€˜what if it is prohibited in the Veda or in the Smá¹›tis? They are not at all authoritativeâ€™;â€”even without saying this, if he should even think in this manner,â€”and if he is found to pay much attention to the science of dialectics;â€”such a person should he cast out by the goodâ€”despised by all cultured personsâ€”out of such acts as â€˜officiating at sacrifices,â€™ â€˜teaching,â€™ â€˜honours of a guestâ€™ and so forth. Since the text does not specify the acts (from which the man should be kept out), it follows that he should be kept out of all those acts that are fit for the learned. And the reason for this lies in the fact that it is only the ignorant man, whose mind is uncultured and who smacks of the polemic, that can speak as above (in deprecation of the Veda); and to the said acts (of officiating, etc.) it is only the learned man that can be entitled. It is in view of this that such â€˜criticismâ€™ has been prohibited in the preceding verse,â€”such criticism being due to want or respect,â€”and it does not deprecate such inquiry as might be instituted for the purpose of elucidating the true meaning of the Veda.

It is in view of all this that the author states the reason for what he has assertedâ€”â€˜The detractor of the Veda being an infidel.â€™ Thus the man, who would set forth arguments in support of the view that â€˜the Veda is unauthoritative,â€™ only by way of a primÃ¢ facie statement, would not he an â€˜infidelâ€™; because such statement of the arguments would he made only for the purpose of strengthening the final conclusion (that the Veda is authoritative).

The text speaking of tin; â€˜detractor of the Veda,â€™ has not mentioned the Smá¹›ti; hut the idea is that both stand on the same footing, and both equally form the subject-matter of the context; hence the mention of any one of them implies both.

Some people might however take the term â€˜Vedaâ€™ (in the expression â€˜detractor of the Vedaâ€™) to be actually restricted to the Veda only, and they would thence conclude that â€˜the detractor of Smá¹›tisâ€™ should not be cast out, the casting out in this verse being declared for the â€˜detractor of the Vedaâ€™ only. With a view to such people the Author adds the following verse.â€”(11)

There is no difference here. The prohibition of detracting the Veda implies that there should be casting out of also one who detracts the Smá¹›ti, the Practic Men and Self-satisfaction. These also describe only such Dharma as is based upon the Veda; hence the detractor of these is also the â€˜detractor of the Veda.â€™

Objection:â€”â€œThere is no need for having both the verses; a single verse would have been sufficient, somewhat to the following effect:â€”â€˜the twice-born person, who, relying upon the Science of Dialectics, should scorn the sources of Dharma, beginning with Veda and ending with Self-satisfaction, should be cast out by all good men, on account of his being an infidel.â€

Our answer to the above is as follows:â€”Teachers do not mind the burdening (prolexity) of their works; what they make every effort to avoid is the burdening of the intellect (of the learner); as it is the latter which interferes with the right understanding of Dharma; and this misunderstanding obstructs the fulfilment of the ends of man.

Then again, even if the author had mentioned all the four sources of Dharma (as suggested by the objector), some people might still argue thusâ€”â€œthe author should have mentioned the Veda only, all Dharma being based upon the Veda.â€ Hence it is for the purpose of clearness that the author has stated the matter in both ways: the former verse being intended for those who like brevity, and both the verses for other persons.

â€˜What is agreeable to oneselfâ€™ is the same as the â€˜self-satisfactionâ€™ spoken of before. The term â€˜oneselfâ€™ is added only for filling up the metre.

â€˜These constitute directly the means of knowing Dharmaâ€™â€”â€˜Laká¹£aá¹‡aâ€™ means cause, indicator; and Sense-Perception is not the means of knowing Dharma, as some people have held, speaking of (the sages as) â€˜persons who have directly perceived Dharma.â€™

In the compound â€˜Chaturvidham,â€™ â€˜fourfold,â€™ the term â€˜vidhÄâ€™ means kind, form. As a matter of fact, Veda is the only source of knowledge of Dharma, and Smá¹›ti and the rest are only so many forms of the Veda.

Other people have explained this second verse as serving the purpose of recapitulating (all that has been said regarding the sources of Dharma). The description of the means of knowing Dharma having been finished, it is the end of this section that is indicated by the repetition (contained in the present verse). Such is the fashion with treatises on the VedÄá¹…gas; e.g., â€˜saá¹ƒsthÄjapenopatiá¹£á¹­hante upatiá¹£á¹­hanteâ€™ (when; â€˜upatiá¹£á¹­hanteâ€™ is repeated for indicating the end of the section); and when the author sets forth the second verse he has in his mind the sum-total of all that has been said in the present section. It is just as the NaiyÄyikas, having propounded the Proposition that â€˜Word is non-eternal,â€™ set forth the reasons in support of it, and then re-iterate the conclusion, saying â€˜therefore word is non-eternal.â€™ In fact such is the way of all writers; e.g., the author of the MahÄbhÄá¹£ya (PatÄÃ±jali) also sometimes states the SÅ«tra or the VÄrtika, and having explained it, repeats it again.â€”(12)

The knowledge of Dharma is ordained for those who are not addicted to the pursuit of wealth and pleasures; and for those seeking for the knowledge of Dharma, the Revealed Word is the highest authority.â€”(13)

â€œWhen the act is endowed with a certain faculty, it does not lose that faculty, even though it may be performed with some other end in view; it must always bring about its natural effects; e.g., even though one may drink poison, with the idea that it is an efficacious medicine, yet it does not fail to kill him. Similarly even though the act may be done with a view to some visible reward, yet it cannot fail to bring about the invisible results mentioned in ’ the scriptures. Why then should you have this aversion, which makes you assert that an act should not be undertaken with a view to worldly advancement?â€

â€˜Wealthâ€™ stands for cattle, land, gold and so forth, and â€˜addictionâ€™ to it means being entirely taken up with the undertaking of cultivation, service, etc., for the purpose of acquiring and accumulating wealth.

â€˜Pleasuresâ€™ stands for sexual pleasures; and â€˜addictionâ€™ to these means constant recourse to it, as also to its accompaniments in the shape of singing and music, etc.

For people who are devoid of these (wealth and pleasures), â€˜the knowledge of Dharma,â€™ the true understanding of Dharma, â€˜is ordained,â€™ specially propounded, accomplished; the verb â€˜vidhÄ«yateâ€™ is derived from the root â€˜dhÄ«á¹…â€™ to accomplish.

Objection.â€”How is it that people addicted to wealth and pleasures can have no knowledge of â€˜Dharma?â€™ In fact such persons also, as time permits them, can obtain some knowledge of â€˜Dharma,â€™â€”by listening at the time of eating and at such times as do not interfere with their pursuit of wealth and pleasure, to stories and to the precept or example of others (who know Dharma).â€

In view of the above objection, the author has added the wordsâ€”â€˜for those who seek the knowledge of Dharma,â€™ etc. The chief authority for Dharma is the Veda; and the Veda can never be understood by the persons referred to. It is extremely difficult to comprehend and for its due comprehension it requires the thorough study of the sciences of Nigama (Vedic commentaries), Nirukla (Philology), VyÄkaraá¹‡a (Grammar), Tarka (Logic) PurÄá¹‡a (History) and MÄ«nÄmsÄ (Exigetics). And this entire mass of literature can never be acquired by a man unless he renounces all other activities. What can be learnt from stories and examples are only a few stray Dharmas, and not the entire body of Dharma, in the shape of the performance of the Jyotiá¹£á¹­oma and other sacrifices, along with all its accessory details, which can be learnt only from the Veda and the other sources of Dharma. It is in view of all this that the text saysâ€”â€˜the Revealed Word is the highest authority.â€™ This how ever is not meant to take away the force of â€˜Exampleâ€™ as a source of knowledge.

What is stated in the text is corroborated by the following well-known sayingâ€”â€˜He alone acquires learning who shuns wealth like snake, sweetmeats like poison, and women like evil spirits?

[ Another explanation of the verse.]

According to others again the expression â€˜arthakÄmaâ€™ stands for hankering after visible rewardsâ€”and for people â€˜addictedâ€™ to these,â€”those who hanker after honour, fame, etc., and who, seeking after visible results, have no other end in life save worldly advancementâ€”â€˜the knowledge of Dharma,â€™ i.e., â€˜performance of Dharma,â€™â€”is not â€˜ordained,â€™ taught. The term â€˜jÃ±Änaâ€™ (â€™knowledgeâ€™) stands forâ€™that in which the act becomes recognisedâ€™ (jÃ±Äyate asmin), i.e., its performance; it is only when the Dharma is performed that it becomes clearly manifest, clearer indeed than that comprehended at the time that the scriptures are studied. It is for this reason that it is the performance that is spoken of as â€˜jÃ±Äna,â€™ â€˜knowledge.â€™

What the text means therefore is this:â€”Even though the performance of Dharma brings worldly advancement, yet one should not undertake it entirely with a view to that fame; it should be undertaken with the sole idea that it is prescribed by the scriptures. The act having been done with this idea, if some visible result also follows, it may do so, but it is not what is thought of by the man. In fact the

Veda itself describes the result following from Vedic study as â€˜fame and worldly advancement,â€™ in the passageâ€”â€˜the world progressing endows him with four thingsâ€”honour, gifts, freedom from taxes and freedom from death.â€™ To the same effect is the following sayingâ€”â€˜The water supplied for the growth of sugar-cane waters also the grasses and creepers, in the same manner when a man treads the path of Dharma, he also obtains fame, pleasure and riches.â€™

Objection.â€”

â€œWhen the act is endowed with a certain faculty, it does not lose that faculty, even though it may be performed with some other end in view; it must always bring about its natural effects; e.g., even though one may drink poison, with the idea that it is an efficacious medicine, yet it does not fail to kill him. Similarly even though the act may be done with a view to some visible reward, yet it cannot fail to bring about the invisible results mentioned in ’ the scriptures. Why then should you have this aversion, which makes you assert that an act should not be undertaken with a view to worldly advancement?â€

It is in view of this objection that the text addsâ€”â€˜For those seeking for the knowledge of Dharma, the Revealed Word is the highest authority.â€™ And what this means is that â€˜those who seek for visible rewards do not obtain any invisible reward,â€”and it is not only that he does not obtain the invisible result, he commits sin also by being addicted to what is prohibited.â€™â€”(13)

The objection urged above (in the BhÄá¹£ya on Verse 10) is answered here.

When â€˜between two Vedic texts there is â€˜conflict,â€™â€”i.e., setting forth of contrary facts,â€”e.g., what is declared to be â€˜Dharmaâ€™ by one text is pronounced to bo â€˜adharmaâ€™ by another;â€”in such cases both are Dharma,â€”i.e., to be performed as optional alternatives. The authoritative strength of the two texts is equal; hence it cannot bo discerned which is reliable and which is not. Thus the conflict being between two equally authoritative texts bearing upon the same subject, there must be option.

Objection.â€”

â€œThe text speaks of both being Dharma, which means that there should be combination (and not option); as it is only when there is combination that both could be Dharma; otherwise (), only one of them could be Dharma. (at a time).â€

We deny this. The use of the word â€˜bothâ€™ is incompatible with separate, performance (even acts performed one after the other, and not conjointly, can be spoken of as â€˜bothâ€™); the word does not necessarily denote two things taken together.

Further, option is distinctly the reasonable course to adopt. The action known as â€˜Agnihotraâ€™ is one only; and with reference to this single act, three points of time have been laid down; now the action forms the primary factor, the comes in only as a subordinate element; nor is it possible to adopt all the three points of time in connection with any single performance; nor again can it be right to repeat the performance for the sake of the time; as it is never right to repeat the primary for the sake of the secondary factor. From all this it follows that option is most reasonable, as declared in the words â€˜whenever there is conflict between authorities of equal force, there is option.â€™ (Gautama, 1.3.)

â€˜Both have bent proclaimed to be Dharma.â€™â€”â€œWhat is the difference between this statement and the former one, â€˜both are Dharmaâ€™?â€

There is no difference; in the former statement, the author has stated his own opinion, and in the latter he supports his opinion by the opinion of other teachers, by pointing out that â€˜this is what has also been proclaimed by other wise men.â€™â€”(14)

This verse is quoted in the Smá¹›ticandrikÄ (Saá¹ƒskÄra, p. 15) as describing the comparative authority of the several sources. Where there are two Vedic texts setting forth two conflicting views, both are to be accepted, since they have been so accepted by authorities older than Manu himself, i. e. the two are to be regarded as optional alternatives.

It is quoted also in the Ná¹›siá¹ƒhaprasÄda (Saá¹ƒskÄra, p. 136.)

This is an instance of the â€˜conflictâ€™ spoken of in the preceding verse.

In regard to the offering of the Agnihotra-lilbtions, all these three points of time have been prescribed,â€”and the injunction of each of these deprecates the others; and the sense of these Vedic texts is that â€˜the merificial act may be performed at any timeâ€™;â€”in all ways the offering proceeds, i.e., it should proceed. As regards the deprecation of the offering done after sunrise (which is found in the text enjoining the time before sunrise), this deprecation is not meant to be an interdict; it is only meant to be an injunction of the offering before sunrise. Similarly in the other cases. Thus what is meant is that the act may be performed at any one of the three points of time; and the command of the scriptures becomes fulfilled by the offering being done at any one time.

â€˜Such is the pronouncement of the Veda.â€™â€”This is what is meant by the Vedic declaration; and it does not mean that what is deprecated is interdicted.

The â€˜sacrificial actâ€™ spoken of here is what is known as the â€˜Agnihotra-homaâ€™; there is not much difference between â€˜yÄgaâ€™(a sacrifice) and â€˜homaâ€™ (libation-pouring). When one renounces his proprietary right over a substance in favour of a certain deity,â€”the idea in his mind being â€˜this belongs to the deity now, and not to me,â€™â€”this is what constitutes â€˜yÄgaâ€™ â€˜sacrificeâ€™; and exactly the same is the form of â€˜Homa,â€™ â€˜Pouring of libation,â€™ also. The only difference is that in Homa there is the additional factor of the substance being thrown, deposited in a specified manner, in fire or some such receptacle. It is in view of this similarity that the Homa is spoken of here as â€˜yajÃ±a.â€™ That this is so is proved by the fact that the three points of time spoken of have been prescribed in the Veda in connection with Homa, and not all kinds of sacrifice.

The expressions â€˜uditeâ€™ (â€˜after sunriseâ€™) and the rest are to be taken as parts of, and as standing for, such declarations as â€˜udite hotavyamâ€™ (â€˜the Homa should be performed after sunriseâ€™) and so forth;â€”the construction being that â€˜the meaning of the declaration that the Homa should be performed after sunrise, not before it, etc., etc., is as follows.â€™

By the compound word â€˜samayÄâ€™ the time of early dawn is meant. Others have taken it as consisting of two words: â€˜samayÄâ€™ meaning near, requires its correlative in the shape of something that is near; and since the two points of time mentioned in the sentence are those â€˜beforeâ€™ and â€˜after sunrise,â€™ the required correlative in the present instance is the time of twilight. â€˜Adhyuá¹£itaâ€™ stands for the time of departure of the night, and means â€˜at the departure of night.â€™ [So the compound means â€˜that twilight which comes after the departure of night.â€™]

The words of the text are intended to quote the words of the Veda, which are found to be read in one form in one text, and in another form in another text; so that whether the expression â€˜samayÄdhyuá¹£itaâ€™ is one word or two words can be ascertained only from the original texts.

Thus then, the same act of Homa being laid down in the Veda as to be done optionally at any one of the three points of time, there is no inconsistency. It is only in connection with two accomplished entities that, when found to be incompatible with one another, they are held to be â€˜inconsistentâ€™; the same cannot be true in connection with things still to he accomplished [and all actions belong to this latter category]. For what has got to be accomplished may be accomplished either in one way or another; and how could there be any inconsistency in this?

In AdhyÄya I (Verse 103) are found the words â€˜this should be studied by the learned BrÄhmaá¹‡aâ€™; and though this is a mere ArthavÄda, yet the presence of the potential affix â€˜tavyaâ€™ (in the term â€˜adhyetavyamâ€™) might lead people into the mistake that it is an Injunction; and in that case the Ká¹£atriya and VaiÅ›ya would be precluded from the study. It is with a view to preclude this possibility that we have the present verse, which shows the capability of the Ká¹£atriya and the VaiÅ›ya also to study.

The older commentators have also taken this verse as serving to preclude the possibility of the ÅšÅ«dra studying the scripture, which he might be tempted to do.

The term â€˜scriptureâ€™ here stands for the compilation by Manu.

â€˜Entitled,â€™â€”the â€˜titleâ€™ meant here consists in the idea that â€˜this should be done by me.â€™ But no â€˜collection of wordsâ€™â€”which is an accomplished entityâ€”can be regarded as â€˜to be doneâ€™; nor, on the other band, can any action be regarded as â€˜to be accomplished,â€™ except as related to a certain substance. Hence the â€˜titleâ€™ is understood to pertain to a certain action relating to the scripture. Now in the present context, the action intended is not found to be either making, or being, or existing; as for â€˜beingâ€™ and â€˜existing,â€™ they both mean â€˜to beâ€™; so that if these were the action meant, the meaning would beâ€”â€˜one should undertake the being or the existing, as related to the scriptureâ€™; but as a matter of fact the â€˜beingâ€™ of one thing cannot be undertaken by another. The action of â€˜makingâ€™ also would not be applicable; because so far as the words are concerned, all are eternal (and as such cannot be made); while as regards sentences, they have been already made by another person (the author of the scripture). From all this it follows that the action intended is that of studying as pertaining to the scripture. So that the meaning comes to be thisâ€”â€˜the man is entitled to the study of the scriptureâ€™; and just as to the studying, so also to the learning of its meaning.

â€œIn as much as the work of Manu has had a beginning in time, how could any injunction relating to it (as the present verse is supposed to be) be based upon the Veda, which is beginningless?â€

Our answer is as follows:â€”It is quite open to us to infer some such general (Vedic) injunction as that â€˜the ÅšÅ«dra should not study such sentences as serve to expound the scriptures.â€™ Further, these statements of the expounders of the Veda that serve to explain Vedic texts bear a certain resemblance to these texts; and hence they are as much the subject of a bcginningless tradition as the Vedic texts themselves.

What forms the subject-matter of the scriptures is actual performance, and to this all the four castes arc entitled [though to the study of the scriptures the three twice-born castes alone are entitled].

â€œIf such be the case, then it would be permissible for the ÅšÅ«dra to perform all those acts which are prescribed in a general manner, without reference to any specified persons.â€

How this contingency does, not arise we shall show, as occasion arises.

Objection:â€”

â€œWhen the ÅšÅ«dra is not entitled to study the scripture and learn its meaning, how can he be entitled to the performance of the acts therein prescribed? Unless the man knows the exact form of the act, he cannot do it; unless he studies the scriptures, he cannot know what is contained in them; and no unlearned person is entitled to the performance of any (religious) act.â€

True; but the requisite knowledge can be obtained from the advice of other persons. The ÅšÅ«dra may be dependent upon a BrÄhmaá¹‡a; or a BrÄhmaá¹‡a may be doing the work of instructing people for payment; and such a BrÄhmaá¹‡a might very well instruct the ÅšÅ«dra to â€˜do this, after having done thatâ€™ and so forth. So that the mere fact of the ÅšÅ«dra performing the acts does not necessarily indicate that he is entitled to the study and understanding of the scriptures; as performance can be accomplished, even on the strength of what is learnt from others; as is done in the case of women; what helps women (in the performance of their duties) is the learning of their husbands, which becomes available to them through companionship. Then again, the texts laying down the acts do not imply the direct knowledge (of the injunctive texts). It is only in the case of men, to whom is addressed the injunction of Vedic studyâ€”contained in the words â€˜one should study the Vedaâ€™â€”that the performance of duties proceeds upon the basis of their own learning; and this injunction is meant only for the male memberâ€™s of the three higher castes. But in the case of these also their study and understanding of the scriptures is not prompted by their knowledge of what is contained in them; it is prompted entirely by the two injunctionsâ€”(1) the injunction of having recourse to a duly qualified teacher, and (2) the injunction of Vedic study.

â€˜Conception,â€™ is â€˜ impregnation â€™; that series of sacraments which begins with this is called â€˜niá¹£Ä“kÄdiá¸¥,â€™ â€˜beyinning conception.â€™ This sacrament is laid down as to be performed after the marriage (of the parents),â€”with the mantra â€˜viá¹£á¹‡uryoniá¹…kalpayatu,â€™ etc. (á¹šgveda, 10.18.1.1)â€”on the occasion of the first intercourse only, for some people, while for others at each menstrual period, till conception takes place.

That which has the â€˜crematoriumâ€™ for its end is called â€˜ending with the crematorium.â€™ The word â€˜crematoriumâ€™ stands for that place where dead bodies are disposed of; and through association it indicates the rites performed for the dead, called the â€˜Antyeá¹£á¹­iâ€™; as it is only this performance that is done with mantras, and not the place (which is what is directly denoted by the word â€˜crematoriumâ€™).

The qualification mentioned in the text points to the Twice-born castes; it is only these persons who have their sacraments done with mantras. The author does not say simply â€˜dvijÄtÄ«nÄm,â€™ â€˜for the twicc-bornâ€™ (and he has taken to the round-about way of saying the same thing), because Manu SvÄyambhuva has a peculiar style of composing his verses.

The passage is not to be construed to mean that â€˜the sacraments are laid down in mantrasâ€™; because mantras do not contain the injunction of any acts; they are not injunctive; they only serve as reminders, during the performance of the act, of the details enjoined in other texts. Hence the passage should be explained as meaningâ€”â€˜those for whom the sacraments are laid down as to be done with Mantras.â€™

â€˜None other,â€™â€”is purely explanatory; the rest of the sentence having already served the purpose of restricting what is said to the twice-born castes. Or, it may be taken as reiterated for the preventing of the idea that some one might entertain the notion that â€˜what is laid down here is something that must be done by the twice-born castes, while for the ÅšÅ«dra it is neither prescribed nor interdicted.â€™â€”(16)

â€˜Mantraiá¸¥â€™â€”This has been added with a view to exclude the woman and the ÅšÅ«dra, whose sacraments are not performed â€˜with mantrasâ€™ (see 2.66 and 10.127).

Burnell remarksâ€”â€˜In Vedic times the reception of outsiders into the community was, to a certain extent, recognised, and ceremonies (e. g. the VrÄtyastoma) were in use for this purpose.â€™
